Woods)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/607333dba451820019b3a75d?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn15@2020_2/2021/04/12/16-44-11-251_36aecf3b7849b4f3.webp","https://i.insider.com/6072e50fa451820019b3a729?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn24@2020_6/2021/04/12/16-44-13-438_08e5863ba6554321.webp","https://i.insider.com/6073375e4943790018bb1d37?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn36@2020_1/2021/04/12/16-44-17-951_aaa4b0f1a937700b.webp","https://i.insider.com/60733b934943790018bb1d38?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn84@2020_6/2021/04/12/16-44-12-321_69ae01b45c3a0e13.webp","https://i.insider.com/60733f2d4943790018bb1d3b?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn61@2020_2/2021/04/12/16-44-17-353_188104e47dd9abb7.webp","https://i.insider.com/6073358f4943790018bb1d32?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn53@2020_3/2021/04/12/16-44-17-409_5fb4422f300282e3.webp","https://i.insider.com/607337f6a451820019b3a763?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn19@2020_6/2021/04/12/16-44-13-900_8865e02d3d1a33ba.webp","https://i.insider.com/607335e2a451820019b3a75e?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn76@2020_1/2021/04/12/16-44-12-476_ef5771c4ad339387.webp","https://i.insider.com/6072e551a451820019b3a72a?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn43@2020_2/2021/04/12/16-44-13-502_0530f28d406a663e.webp","https://i.insider.com/60733fbf4943790018bb1d3c?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn72@2020_3/2021/04/12/16-44-12-692_7154434199e76710.webp","https://i.insider.com/6072e5d4a451820019b3a72b?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn63@2020_5/2021/04/12/16-44-13-696_5b28d91186be92a4.webp","https://i.insider.com/60733c01a451820019b3a764?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn13@2020_1/2021/04/12/16-44-18-357_3e7f29fad6b7744d.webp","https://i.insider.com/60733c484943790018bb1d39?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn52@2020_2/2021/04/12/16-44-13-406_1900fc13a9af6c4f.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn39@2020_2/2021/04/12/16-44-16-326_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=PelUvQL4f1o:xSzZv9vHOoM:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn60@2020_2/2021/04/12/16-44-11-983_329fb1f255a71f0d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=PelUvQL4f1o:xSzZv9vHOoM:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn80@2020_5/2021/04/12/16-44-16-115_802a3f228b979be0.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn51@2020_2/2021/04/12/16-44-13-888_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=PelUvQL4f1o:xSzZv9vHOoM:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn27@2020_1/2021/04/12/16-44-11-855_f496a894cf98c15c.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/PelUvQL4f1o":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn71@2020_1/2021/04/12/16-44-13-069_d575c10bbc20cb69.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1618245786412},{"createdTime":"2021-04-13 00:43:06","updatedTime":"2021-04-13 00:43:06","title":"A 35-year CIA veteran explains what China's up to around Taiwan and what the US should do about it","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/cia-veteran-on-china-plans-for-taiwan-and-us-response-2021-4","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f0dc10cf0f4197853590bea?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Taiwan Tien Chien missile\" height=\"1100\" width=\"2200\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Taiwanese soldiers carry a surface-to-air missile to a launcher during a military exercise at an air force base in Chiayi, southern Taiwan, August 24, 2010.\n<p class=\"copyright\">REUTERS/Nicky Loh</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Rising tensions between China and Taiwan, including increasing Chinese military activity, have US officials worried about a conflict.</li>\n<li>John Culver, a 35-year CIA officer and former national intelligence officer for East Asia, explains the risk of future conflict in the Taiwan Strait, how Taiwan is responding to rising pressure from China, and steps the US could take to support Taiwan.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p><strong>Editor&#39;s Note:</strong> <em>This piece is part of the </em><a href=\"https://www.brookings.edu/series/taiwan-u-s-quarterly-analysis/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>Taiwan-U.S. Quarterly Analysis series</em></a><em>, which features the original writings of experts from the United States and Taiwan, with the goal of providing a range of perspectives on developments relating to Taiwan</em>.</p><p>John Culver retired in 2020 after a distinguished 35-year career at the Central Intelligence Agency. During that time, he analyzed East Asian affairs, including security, economic, and foreign policy dimensions.</p><p>As national intelligence officer for East Asia from 2015 to 2018, he drove the intelligence community&#39;s support to top policymakers on East Asian issues. He routinely participated in meetings at the White House, with leaders throughout the United States government, and with foreign government officials.</p><p>In a conversation with Brookings Senior Fellow and interim Koo Chair in Taiwan Studies Ryan Hass, the two discussed the risk of future conflict in the Taiwan Strait, how Taiwan is responding to rising pressure from China, and steps the United States could take to support Taiwan in consideration of its interests.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f50213ce6ff30001d4e6c57?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Shiyu Kinmen County Taiwan China\" height=\"1673\" width=\"2500\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Shiyu, or Lion Islet, one of Taiwan&#39;s offshore islands, seen in front of the Chinese city of Xiamen, April 20, 2018.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Carl Court/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p><strong>Ryan Hass: How do you see cross-Strait issues playing in China&#39;s domestic politics? Is Taiwan policy a high priority or a source of debate? And do you expect the role of Taiwan in China&#39;s politics to change as the 20th Congress of the Chinese Communist Party (CCP) draws nearer in fall 2022?</strong></p><p><strong>John Culver</strong>: One of my core assumptions is that things said by the CCP about Taiwan are generally not directed solely or principally at the Taiwan public, but instead at China&#39;s own domestic population, or at the US government and a few other foreign governments - principally Tokyo and Canberra.</p><p>This reflects the fact that Taipei has not taken highly provocative or precipitous actions since 2008, when then-President Chen Shui-bian stirred controversy by advocating, through a public referendum, for Taipei to pursue UN membership under the name &#34;Taiwan.&#34;</p><p>In this vein, I don&#39;t expect the role of Taiwan in China&#39;s politics to change due solely - or even mostly - to the 20th Party Congress in 2022, unless provoked by Taiwan&#39;s own election cycle, which would be gearing up for the January 2024 polls.</p><p>Thinking back over the past 30 to 40 years, we&#39;ve seen street demonstrations in China (officially the People&#39;s Republic of China, or PRC) - some violent - over Japan and domestic local issues (land use, pollution, ethnic strife). Some have even been directed at the United States, after events like the 1999 accidental bombing of the Chinese embassy in Belgrade or the 2001 People&#39;s Liberation Army (PLA) Navy fighter collision with a US EP-3 reconnaissance aircraft. But I don&#39;t recall ever seeing a major protest in China over Taiwan policy, which to me says that the PRC public isn&#39;t on tenterhooks over the issue and generally thinks that the CCP&#39;s policies toward Taiwan are &#34;tough enough&#34; and correct.</p><p>In other words, President Xi Jinping and the CCP are not facing much domestic pressure to do something, or at least to do something different. But galvanizing Chinese public opinion to justify harsher policies is a card Beijing could always play - we should keep in mind that it has not.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f501e89e6ff30001d4e6c54?format=jpeg\" alt=\"China amphibious tanks invasion\" height=\"1694\" width=\"2500\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Chinese Army amphibious tanks land on a beach during a Sino-Russian military exercise near China&#39;s Shandong Peninsula, August 24, 2005.\n<p class=\"copyright\">China Photos/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p><strong>RH: Part of China&#39;s strategy appears to be placing psychological stress upon the people of Taiwan, both to raise perceptions of the costs and risks of independence and also to seed the idea that unification ultimately is inevitable. Do you agree? If so, how (in)effective do you China&#39;s efforts have been?</strong></p><p><strong>JC</strong>: Taiwan is an issue that the CCP sees as a threat to its legitimacy, not an opportunity to be seized. That has meant that CCP policy toward Taiwan is largely about what it wants to avoid, not what it wants to achieve - reactionary, not exploitative.</p><p>But that is changing, especially since Xi in 2019 and more recently has framed &#34;reunification&#34; as a requirement for achieving the &#34;China Dream&#34; tied to the CCP&#39;s longstanding goals for 2049, the 100th anniversary of the PRC&#39;s founding. We should worry that Xi may decide to take risks that his more constrained predecessors since Mao Zedong would not.</p><p>I don&#39;t think China today actually seeks to place severe psychological stress on Taiwan. Most of its actions are more formal and symbolic - like the current &#34;<a href=\"https://finance.yahoo.com/news/china-taiwan-face-off-pineapple-220804121.html?guccounter=1\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">pineapple boycott</a>&#34; - and are meant to stay ahead of Chinese domestic opinion, or warn against exploitation by the United States or another outside power.</p><p>Beijing is rigorous about cataloging and protesting every &#34;improper&#34; action by the United States, Taiwan, or occasionally by third parties, to ensure that it is vocal in opposition to continued erosion. And yet, by most measures, the situation continues to erode. For Washington, dating back almost a decade now, there are diminishing incentives to &#34;please Beijing&#34; by <em>not</em> doing something with Taiwan - they&#39;re going to protest anyway, even to modest arms sales, official visits, etc.</p><p>Some have interpreted President Xi&#39;s 2017 19th Party Congress report as setting a deadline for reunification by 2049, <a href=\"https://www.chinadaily.com.cn/china/19thcpcnationalcongress/2017-11/04/content_34115212.htm\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">when he stated</a> that &#34;complete national reunification is an inevitable requirement for realizing the great rejuvenation of the Chinese nation.&#34;</p><p>I approach the &#34;deadline&#34; interpretation with caution, since there&#39;s a pattern of previous leaders setting somewhat vague deadlines or timelines in order to look tough about &#34;solving&#34; the problem while at the same time kicking the can down the road for the successors. In other words, &#34;deadlines for unification&#34; typically have been driven more by internal CCP leadership dynamics and legacy-making than ordering a firm set of multi-domain operations to commence to produce something the CCP can call &#34;unification&#34; by a certain date.</p><p>I don&#39;t dismiss Xi&#39;s 2049 framing for the party&#39;s lofty ambitions that would include Taiwan. At some point in the not-too-distant future, perhaps 2030 or 2035, the PLA probably will have the organizational and warfighting capacity for a Taiwan operation that it has always lacked. China will probably be the largest economy, and an even more dominant trading and advanced manufacturing powerhouse. Weakness could no longer be an excuse for an increasingly nationalistic population that has only known China&#39;s rise.</p><p>As I noted <a href=\"https://www.lowyinstitute.org/the-interpreter/unfinished-chinese-civil-war\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">in an article last year</a> about China&#39;s &#34;unfinished civil war,&#34; the CCP&#39;s ultimate objective isn&#39;t invasion but instead a process between China and Taiwan authorities to negotiate the formal, long-term political relationship across the strait. Military, economic, information, and diplomatic coercion and inducements would all be in play, and the red line for threatened military force would shift from preventing permanent separation to a refusal by Taipei to begin the political process - <a href=\"http://www.china-embassy.org/eng/zt/999999999/t187406.htm\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">there is language in the 2005 anti-secession law along these lines</a>.</p><p>If 2049 is the CCP&#39;s goal for beginning a formal unification process, China&#39;s cross-strait policies would clearly break from the past sometime after 2030. China&#39;s proposals initially could be fairly lenient, <a href=\"http://www.china.org.cn/english/taiwan/7943.htm\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">not dissimilar from the CCP&#39;s 1979 letter to the Taiwan compatriots</a>, but a key condition would be the end of a US-Taiwan security framework without Beijing&#39;s explicit approval.</p><p>Perhaps a longer-term US goal, short of war, should be a strategy designed to change the CCP&#39;s definition of &#34;unification&#34; to something like a commonwealth or confederation, or even one similar to the US-Canada arrangement.</p><p>But even that would be a fraught domestic political step for the United States. It would require a consensus here that &#34;solving&#34; the Taiwan issue should even be our goal. Like so many of our culture-war issues in the US, there are powerful interests that are deeply invested in not solving these problems.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/58759233ee14b62b008b7a8d?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Tsai Ing-wen\" height=\"3264\" width=\"4896\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Taiwanese President Tsai Ing-wen during a visit in Managua, Nicaragua, January 10, 2017.\n<p class=\"copyright\"><a href=\"http://pictures.reuters.com/archive/NICARAGUA-TAIWAN--RC1FE1166590.html\" target=\"_blank\">Oswaldo Rivas/Reuters</a></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p><strong>RH: How do you expect Taiwan will respond to rising Chinese pressure in the next year?</strong></p><p><strong>JC</strong>: It&#39;s become very common in the United States to frame Taiwan as the embattled, doughty democracy withstanding building pressure from the authoritarian mainland lusting to launch an invasion. We seem to yearn for a second cold war, because we think we know how to &#34;fight&#34; and win that scenario, so it&#39;s tempting to cast Taiwan as another Hungary or Czechoslovakia.</p><p>In reality, and in the history of the past 40 years, Taiwan has emerged as a vibrant democracy, and polling indicates that most Taiwanese don&#39;t feel very threatened - there&#39;s little domestic pressure to massively increase military spending or return to lengthy universal conscription.</p><p>Most Taiwan people aren&#39;t worried about imminent attack, because 40 years since US derecognition of the Republic of China (ROC) have passed without war, but also because some think that Taiwan could not prevail without massive US military intervention so there&#39;s little point in building up Taiwan&#39;s own military.</p><p>The Taiwan public also continues to mistrust its military as an institution because of the ROC military&#39;s standing as a pro-Kuomintang (KMT) mainlander-dominated stronghold.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f0dbe8a4dca68713f141422?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Taiwan navy frigate missile\" height=\"1665\" width=\"2500\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A Taiwanese frigate fires an anti-submarine missile during an exercise near Hualien, Taiwan, May 22, 2019.\n<p class=\"copyright\">REUTERS/Tyrone Siu</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p><strong>RH: There is growing talk in the United States about the rising risk of conflict in the Taiwan Strait. US Indo-Pacific Command commander Philip Davidson recently <a href=\"https://www.nbcnews.com/news/world/china-could-invade-taiwan-next-6-years-assume-global-leadership-n1260386\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">expressed concern</a> publicly about the potential for conflict in the next six years. Where do you fall in this debate?</strong></p><p><strong>JC</strong>: I disagree with the tone and import of recent public pronouncements by Davidson or former National Security Advisor <a href=\"https://asia.nikkei.com/Politics/International-relations/Taiwan-in-danger-from-2022-on-expert-warns-US-Congress\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">H.R. McMaster</a> that war is likely in a defined span of a few years.</p><p>Intentionally or not, they convey that China has a fixed timeline and agenda for compelling Taiwan, whether based on some assumption that as soon as the PLA is &#34;ready,&#34; China will launch an invasion, or that the CCP will launch an opportunistic war to shore up domestic legitimacy. None of this is true in terms of China&#39;s goals, its view of the usefulness of military force, or how the CCP&#39;s legitimacy has been trending or is likely to trend over the decade.</p><p>I&#39;m not predicting or forecasting that war <em>won&#39;t</em> happen. I&#39;m just saying that war isn&#39;t &#34;the plan&#34; for the CCP. The real danger is that all of the factors that tended to preserve the status quo since US-China diplomatic recognition in 1979 have eroded and are likely to continue to erode.</p><p>These include the military balance - which has swung decisively in China&#39;s favor - but in many ways that&#39;s the least consequential change in and of itself because even now, China is not building the invasion fleet. The more destabilizing factors driving the dynamic are:</p><ul><li>Taiwan&#39;s domestic political and identity development, where even the KMT is unlikely to sustain its prior position on the &#34;<a href=\"https://www.brookings.edu/blog/order-from-chaos/2016/04/22/cross-strait-relations-not-a-one-way-street/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">1992 consensus</a>.&#34; To avoid political oblivion, the KMT continues to transition into a fully Taiwan-centric party that must appeal to domestic sentiment, which is turning even more strongly against any form of unification under any timeline.</li><li>The emergence of full-blown US-China strategic rivalry, which increases Taiwan&#39;s attraction to both major US political parties as a litmus test of &#34;standing up to China.&#34; There&#39;s a myth that the main constraint against Taiwan independence has been the threat of Chinese military action. At least since the mid-1990s, the main constraint against more independence-focused policies and election outcomes on Taiwan has been pressure on Taipei by Washington - this is really clear from US policies and actions over the course of the Chen Shui-bian and Ma Ying-jeou administrations. We are much more incentivized today and looking forward to &#34;play the Taiwan card&#34; due to our own bipartisan political dynamic than because of actions by even an explicitly pro-independence leader in Taipei (which President Tsai Ing-wen is not).</li><li>China&#39;s own emergence as a great power with clear military dominance over Taiwan and seeming parity versus the US. The CCP no longer has the excuse of not acting violently because it is &#34;weak.&#34; Chinese domestic public opinion has grown more nationalistic as its relative governance success compared to the United States throughout the trade war and the pandemic have played out.</li></ul><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60632b08c9d73b00186e91c8?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Palau Taiwan ambassador John Hennessey-Niland\" height=\"1333\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Taiwan Foreign Minister Joseph Wu, left, Palau President Surangel Whipps, center, and US Ambassador to Palau John Hennessey-Niland in Taipei, March 29, 2021.\n<p class=\"copyright\">REUTERS/Ann Wang</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p><strong>RH: In the face of rising pressure from China on Taiwan, how should the United States most effectively signal support and reassurance to Taiwan in a manner that advances US interests?</strong></p><p><strong>JC</strong>: Foremost, the United States should try to separate its growing appetite for great power competition with China as much as possible from Taiwan policy. For me this means not falling victim to the recent preoccupation with imminent conflict due to the designs of the CCP.</p><p>Washington&#39;s actions to enhance Taiwan&#39;s freedom from Chinese coercion should be driven by substance, not symbolism. If the United States previously erred on the side of &#34;pleasing China&#34; by treating Taipei in ways that Taipei found insulting, it should resist the strong temptation to take actions primarily to antagonize and insult Beijing.</p><p>US policy for Taiwan should follow the Tsai administration&#39;s example of basing its legitimacy on the vibrant quality of its democracy and economic freedom. There are an array of steps the United States can take with regard to Taiwan on trade, multinational democratic forums, health policy, and even security affairs that neither stretch Washington&#39;s standard invocation of the <a href=\"https://www.ait.org.tw/our-relationship/policy-history/key-u-s-foreign-policy-documents-region/taiwan-relations-act/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Three Communiques, the Taiwan Relations Act</a>, and other assurances nor risk abandonment.</p><p>And especially with regard to US security policy toward Taiwan, cooperation with Taipei should focus on steps that make a use-of-force decision by Beijing a difficult one, fraught with risk, that relies less on big-ticket, budget-busting weapons purchases and more on public determination and capacity to resist military force.</p><p>This should include US advice and assistance to enhance the credibility of Taiwan&#39;s military as a <em>Taiwanese</em> institution, not a legacy of the Chinese civil war that enforced martial law on the island until the late 1980s. Building public trust in the Taiwan military is essential to overcome lack of serious focus on the threat of military conflict and defeatism.</p><p><em>All statements of fact, opinion, or analysis expressed are those of the author and do not reflect the official position or views of the US government. Nothing in the contents should be construed as asserting or implying US government authentication of information or endorsement of the author&#39;s views.</em></p><p><em>Adrien Chorn provided editing assistance on this piece.</em></p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a class=\"copyright\">Domino's/Nuro.</p> \n  </figcaption> </img>\n</figure>\n<ul class=\"summary-list\"> \n <li>Domino's is delivering pizzas in Houston in an autonomous robot.</li> \n <li>Nuro, the robotics company, is also partnered with CVS, Walmart, and Chipotle.</li> \n <li>Delivery has been huge for restaurants during the pandemic, and chains like Domino's surged ahead.</li> \n <li href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/self-driving-delivery-vehicle-startup-nuro-how-it-works-2020-11#its-latest-500-million-round-saw-new-investors-like-baillie-gifford-and-fidelity-management-and-research-as-well-as-existing-investors-like-greylock-partners-and-softbank-vision-fund-1-2\">Nuro</a>, the companies announced.</p>\n<p>The program will be available to certain customers in Houston, Texas using Nuro's R2, the first autonomous on-road delivery vehicle approved by the US Department of Transportation.</p>\n<p>Customers can order pizza online and opt to receive their delivery through Nuro. Then, they'll get texts with information about the vehicle's location and a PIN to enter on the touchscreen and get their pizza.</p>\n<p><em><strong>Read more: </strong><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/chipotle-outlines-its-strategy-for-deploying-nuro-autonomous-vehicles-2021-4\" data-e2e-name=\"inline-read-more-link\">Chipotle's chief technology officer reveals the chain's strategy for deploying Nuro's autonomous vehicles for burrito-bowl deliveries and more</a></em></p>\n<p>Domino's senior vice president and chief innovation officer Dennis Maloney says Nuro is part of the evolution of delivery.</p>\n<p>\"This program will allow us to better understand how customers respond to the deliveries, how they interact with the robot and how it affects store operations,\" Maloney said in a statement. \"The growing demand for great-tasting pizza creates the need for more deliveries, and we look forward to seeing how autonomous delivery can work along with Domino's existing delivery experts to better support the customers' needs.\"</p>\n<figure> \n <img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60744d6f2cc1ef0018da1307?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Nuro x Domino's Image 1\" height=\"2663\" width=\"3551\" charset=\"\"> \n  <figcaption> \n   <p class=\"copyright\">Domino's/Nuro</p> \n  </figcaption> </img>\n</figure>\n<p>Pizza and wings were hailed as early winners in the pandemic as Americans increasingly ordered from brands that were already set up to accommodate delivery, like <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/what-americans-are-eating-and-drinking-during-quarantine-2020-5#chicken-wings-8\" data-analytics-module=\"body_link\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"40\" data-uri=\"b368180e9f223d736892025037c4a79b\">Papa John's and Wingstop.</a> Domino's added over <a href=\"https://dominos.gcs-web.com/static-files/9b5b591b-d769-4ff3-9521-f6e28548c5ea\">600 new stores</a> in 2020, overall sales were up 10.4% for the year. Meanwhile, the rest of the industry saw huge losses, up to $30 billion in March 2020 and <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/american-quarantine-diet-boost-sales-at-wingstop-papa-johns-2020-5\" data-analytics-module=\"body_link\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"40\" data-uri=\"b3649dc1bbb7693a9625dfb09d49ff04\">$50 billion in April 2020.</a></p>\n<p>Sales via delivery, curbside pickup, and <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/starbucks-drive-thru-menu-changes-customization-orders-2021-3\">drive-thrus</a> all exploded for restaurants during the pandemic as <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/california-covid-restrictions-ending-june-restaurants-bars-movie-theaters-2021-4\">indoor dining closed</a> and consumers avoided shopping inside restaurants and <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/coronavirus-pandemic-could-popularize-grocery-and-food-delivery-2020-3\">grocery stores.</a></p>\n<p>California-based Nuro is using this delivery push as a chance to expand. So far, the company has partnered with <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/self-driving-delivery-vehicle-startup-nuro-how-it-works-2020-11#according-to-a-report-by-techcrunch-nuro-now-has-a-5-billion-valuation-1\">Walmart, CVS, Kroger</a>, and <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/chipotle-outlines-its-strategy-for-deploying-nuro-autonomous-vehicles-2021-4\">Chipotle.</a></p>\n<p>\"Nuro's mission is to better everyday life through robotics. Now, for the first time, we're launching real world, autonomous deliveries with R2 and Domino's,\" co-founder and president Dave Ferguson said in a statement.</p>\n<p><em>Do you have a story to share about a retail or restaurant chain? The exchange said private market transactions valued the firm at <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/currencies/news/coinbase-ipo-valued-68-billion-ahead-direct-listing-bitcoin-cryptocurrency-2021-3-1030220737?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestgest\">about $68 billion</a> in March.</p><p>D&#39;Anethan added: &#34;Coinbase&#39;s IPO is definitely a supportive move for the space as it is bolstering the legitimacy of the asset class and offering investors new ways to interact with it.&#34;</p><p>Edward Moya, senior market analyst at currency firm Oanda, said in an email &#34;a disappointing IPO or excessive concerns over enhanced regulatory oversight could weigh on bitcoin and the other altcoins.&#34;</p><p>Bitcoin has more than doubled in 2021 thanks to a renewed interest in digital currencies supported by huge amounts of stimulus <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stimulus-biden-signs-trillion-covid-relief-package-checks-direct-payments-2021-3\">from governments</a> and central banks.</p><p>Now, <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/currencies/news/bitcoin-btc-institutional-interest-cryptocurrencies-wall-street-tesla-microstrategy-jpmorgan-2021-3-1030194067\">many major institutions</a> are moving into the crypto space, adding legitimacy to bitcoin and other currencies.</p><p>Yet cryptocurrencies continue to divide the financial world, with many figures saying they are <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/bitcoin-price-volatility-forecast-limited-tradability-analysis-deutsche-bank-2021-3\">too volatile</a> to become serious investments for major players. href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?a=qeqwMMaEJRw:pSfkc-V06WE:gIN9vFwOqvQ\"><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=qeqwMMaEJRw:pSfkc-V06WE:gIN9vFwOqvQ\" border=\"0\"></img></a>\n</div><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/qeqwMMaEJRw\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Mon, 12 Apr 2021 15:47:07 +0000","feedId":22,"bgimg":"https://i.insider.com/60746b533e3fe7001882a1cc?format=jpeg","linkMd5":"057f70e6b7c926d9137b09412aed3a83","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn80@2020_6/2021/04/12/16-44-11-060_c9d9f6ac644161cd.webp","destWidth":3565,"destHeight":2674,"sourceBytes":651408,"destBytes":651408,"author":"hrobertson@businessinsider.com (Harry Robertson)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/60746b593e3fe7001882a1cd?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn32@2020_1/2021/04/12/16-44-14-036_f73b554536d20504.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn39@2020_2/2021/04/12/16-44-16-326_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=qeqwMMaEJRw:pSfkc-V06WE:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn12@2020_5/2021/04/12/16-44-11-683_7a577b7b876b267c.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=qeqwMMaEJRw:pSfkc-V06WE:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn67@2020_2/2021/04/12/16-44-18-028_d4473c763707d6c5.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn51@2020_2/2021/04/12/16-44-13-888_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=qeqwMMaEJRw:pSfkc-V06WE:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn64@2020_4/2021/04/12/16-44-12-208_88c94edc6d33c22f.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/qeqwMMaEJRw":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn32@2020_3/2021/04/12/16-44-11-726_48b798aa33054848.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1618245786395},{"createdTime":"2021-04-13 00:43:06","updatedTime":"2021-04-13 00:43:06","title":"What is the 80/20 rule? Understanding the economic meaning of the Pareto Principle","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/80-20-rule","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60746e352cc1ef0018da13aa?format=jpeg\" alt=\"GettyImages 1207388625\" height=\"3360\" width=\"6720\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The 8/20 rule is also called the Pareto Principle or the &#34;law of the vital few.&#34;\n<p class=\"copyright\"><a href=\"https://media.gettyimages.com/photos/man-working-in-home-office-picture-id1207388625?k=6&#38;m=1207388625&#38;s=612x612&#38;w=0&#38;h=_UXjQ2r-3rvhPS-_p9suFNRf3ytQYyOCJv6GFQ6MC1U=\" target=\"_blank\">MoMo Productions/Getty Images</a></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>The 80/20 rule, also known as the Pareto Principle, finds that 80% of the effects come from 20% of the causes for any given situation. </li>\n<li>The 80/20 rule can apply to a wide range of fields, but is most commonly used in business and economics.</li>\n<li>Professionals advise against using the 80/20 rule to guide investing because it can distract from specific long-term goals.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/investing-reference\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\" data-analytics-module=\"summary_bullets\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"0\" data-uri=\"1b4b1b102a76bd3c1b16e44162761b54\"><strong>Visit Business Insider&#39;s Investing Reference library for more stories.</strong></a></li>\n</ul><p>If you&#39;ve ever noticed that a few key players in your portfolio seem to be guiding most of its success, you might have been on to something. This idea is known as the 80/20 rule, which states that 80% of a situation&#39;s outputs result from only 20% of inputs. To put it in simpler terms, it means the majority of results come from a minority of causes. </p><p>Although it&#39;s most commonly applied to business and economics, the 80/20 rule can also be applied to fields like investing and personal finance. Professionals advise against trying to use this rule to actively guide investment strategy, since the stock market can be so unpredictable, but find that it can hold true when evaluating or reflecting on past investments. </p><h2>What is the 80/20 rule?</h2><p>Referred to as the Pareto Principle after Italian economist Vilfredo Pareto, the 80/20 rule finds that 80% of the outcomes or results in a given situation stem from only 20% of what went into it. It&#39;s often used to identify the most efficient way of doing things and focus on developing them to maximize productivity.</p><p>The 80/20 rule can help individuals either identify and target problem areas and refine current strategies, or understand where a process or input is doing especially well and work to replicate it elsewhere. </p><aside class=\"breakout-box headline-regular\"><h2><strong>Where does the 80/20 rule apply?</strong></h2><ul><li aria-level=\"1\"><strong>In the business setting:</strong> This principle has been used to evaluate and improve management (when 20% of employees produce 80% of results), sales strategies (20% of customers bring in 80% sales) and operations (80% of product defects come from 20% of production problems).</li><li aria-level=\"1\"><strong>In personal finance: </strong>The 80/20 rule is often used to guide budgeting. It directs individuals to put 20% of their monthly income into savings, whether that&#39;s a traditional savings account or a brokerage or retirement account, to ensure that there&#39;s enough set aside in the event of financial difficulty, and use the remaining 80% as expendable income.</li><li aria-level=\"1\"><strong>In investing:</strong> It&#39;s been found that 20% of a portfolio&#39;s holdings often lead to 80% of its growth. The opposite can also be true, with 80% of investment losses tracing back to 20% of holdings. But because of the stock market&#39;s unpredictable nature, this rule is often seen as an effective way to evaluate past investments instead of guide future ones.</li></ul></aside><h2>Understanding the 80/20 rule</h2><p>The 80/20 rule first originated when Pareto observed that 20% of the pea pods in his garden yielded 80% of its peas. He went on to apply the concept on a much broader scale, noting that 20% of Italy&#39;s population owned 80% of its wealth. Since then, the concept has been applied to business strategies, software development, healthcare, and more.</p><p>It should be noted that the 80/20 rule is not a strict or definite mathematical law, and is backed by more anecdotal evidence than scientific analysis. It&#39;s mere coincidence that the two numbers add up to 100%, and the inputs (80%) and outputs (20%) are simply meant to represent different units rather than be used to guide precise calculations.</p><h2>What does the 80/20 rule mean for my portfolio?</h2><p>Though its applications can be widely observed, investment professionals advise against trying to apply the 80/20 rule when building a portfolio. </p><p>The 80/20 rule can be effectively used to guard against risk when individuals put 80% of their money into safer investments, like savings bonds and CDs, and the remaining 20% into riskier growth stocks. However, using the 80/20 rule to try and hand-pick stocks that will potentially yield 80% of your returns is ill-advised.</p><p>&#34;It tells you about history, it doesn&#39;t tell you about the future - nobody knows the future,&#34; says <a href=\"https://www.jillonmoney.com/about\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Jill Schlesinger</a>, CBS business analyst and host of Jill on Money. &#34;80% of the people who hit their goals concentrate on their goals, not on their investments.&#34;</p><p>In this regard, the 80/20 rule is most relevant as a metric for evaluation, not prediction.</p><p>&#34;Upon reflection, you [may be able to] look back and say, &#39;You know what, for the forty years that I was an investor, 80% of my returns came from 20% of my portfolio.&#39; You&#39;re probably not going to know until after the fact, or a period of time, when you can see what that 20% was,&#34; says Schlesinger.</p><p>It can also be argued that if only 20% of the investments in an equity portfolio are contributing to 80% of its gains, it&#39;s a rather poor portfolio allocation. </p><p>Generally speaking, each investment in your portfolio should serve a specific purpose and contribute toward the overall goal, whether that&#39;s investing for growth, risk-adjustment, or diversification. Placing too much emphasis on which equities might spur the most growth can distract investors from the bigger picture.</p><h2>The financial takeaway</h2><p>The 80/20 rule finds that most (80%) of a situation or process&#39;s results come from only a few (20%) of its causes. This rule can be applied in a diverse range of fields, but investment professionals advise against using this principle to guide portfolio decisions. </p><p>Instead of using the 80/20 rule to try and curate a portfolio where a few investments will shine, it&#39;s best to establish clear, quantitative investment goals with a diversified portfolio to guard against risk.</p><bi-shortcode it is paying non-employee board members entirely in <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/currencies/btc-usd?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestgest\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">bitcoin</a> instead of cash.</p><p>Company founder and CEO Michael Saylor made the announcement on <a href=\"https://twitter.com/michael_saylor/status/1381579120354717699?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw%7Ctwcamp%5Etweetembed%7Ctwterm%5E1381579120354717699%7Ctwgr%5E%7Ctwcon%5Es1_c10&ref_url=https%3A%2F%2Fdecrypt.co%2F66190%2Fmicrostrategy-board-of-directors-now-paid-in-bitcoincoin\">Twitter</a>, approving bitcoin as a form of compensation for Board service, the Board cited its commitment to bitcoin given its ability to serve as a store of value, supported by a robust and public open-source architecture, untethered to sovereign monetary policy,&#34; the firm said.</p><p>Under the new arrangement, the compensation will be converted to bitcoin from US dollars and will be deposited into the non-employee directors&#39; digital wallets. The fees payable are unchanged.</p><p>The move was cheered on by bitcoin bulls such as <a href=\"https://twitter.com/APompliano/status/1381579688880001024\">Anthony Pompliano</a>, who called Saylor a legend, and <a href=\"https://twitter.com/tyler/status/1381276931614736387\">Tyler Winklevoss,</a> who said bitcoin is &#34;the ultimate exit strategy.&#34;</p><p>Saylor&#39;s announcement follows the company&#39;s last $15 million bitcoin purchase on <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/currencies/news/michael-saylor-microstrategy-buys-15-million-of-bitcoin-btc-2021-4-1030274270\">April 5</a>. The business intelligence firm&#39;s total bitcoin holdings are now worth almost $5.29 billion.</p><p>MicroStrategy, the first corporation to directly purchase bitcoin, made its first $250 million investment in August of 2020 and announced that it was using existing cash on its balance sheet to acquire more of the digital currency.</p><p>Saylor has long been an advocate of digital currencies, especially bitcoin, and has been vocal about his stance. 00:43:06","updatedTime":"2021-04-13 00:43:06","title":"The Archegos meltdown will result in a $10 billion loss for global banks, JPMorgan src=\"https://i.insider.com/6022eea7cca90200129b74e6?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Wall Street.\" height=\"2002\" width=\"4005\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Big Tech recovers after a rough day Wednesday on Wall Street.\n<p class=\"copyright\"><a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/detail/photo/new-york-stock-exchange-wall-st-new-york-usa-royalty-free-image/668600179?adppopup=true\" target=\"_blank\">Matteo Colombo/Getty Images</a></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Global banks are expected to lose up to $10 billion from the Archegos meltdown, JPMorgan said.</li>\n<li>This is 5x the normal loss level for a collateralized daily mark-to-market business, JPMorgan added.</li>\n<li>It however cited three lessons the industry could take away from the implosion that has roiled the markets. </li>\n<li><a href=\"https://newsletter.businessinsider.com/join/4np/10-things-opening-bell\">Sign up here for our daily newsletter, 10 Things Before the Opening Bell</a></li>\n</ul><p>Global banks are expected to lose up to $10 billion following the Archegos Capital Management meltdown, JPMorgan said Monday - raising its estimate from an initial $2 billion-$5 billion - with Credit Suisse Group and Nomura Holding hardest hit.</p><p>&#34;One line of argument which could explain why the scale of losses suffered by [Credit Suisse] and Nomura was higher could be a higher level of leverage extended by these banks compared to [Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley], which seem to have suffered smaller losses if any,&#34; JPMorgan analysts led by Kian Abouhossein said in a research note Monday. </p><p>JPMorgan clarified that there may also be additional considerations that determined the sizable difference between the scale of losses suffered, such as the timing of the sale of positions, among others. Nonetheless, the entire episode affects the industry overall, given that global banks could end up losing five times the normal loss level for a collateralized daily mark-to-market business.</p><p>JPMorgan cites three lessons the industry could take away from the fund&#39;s implosion. </p><p>First, investment banks in general are in better shape today and are more focused on high-volume execution platforms.</p><p>&#34;There is no excessive leverage in the [investment banking] or [private banking] industry,&#34; JPMorgan said.  &#34;Although [private banking] leverage has been increasing, it is nowhere near prior peaks.&#34;</p><p>The bank also said it sees no excessive equity-swap growth, a simple instrument all parties will benefit from.  </p><p>Second, US regulatory frameworks like Basel III and the Dodd-Frank Act have improved the risk profile of investment banks. JPMorgan, however, noted that there is still weak oversight for non-bank entities, especially when it comes to family offices.</p><p>Archegos, a <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/bill-hwang-lost-8-billion-during-archegos-meltdown-reports-say-2021-4-1030272030\">family office</a> founded in 2012, did not have to disclose investments, unlike traditional hedge funds. JPMorgan also pointed to the lack of transparency when it came to equity-swap filings.</p><p>The Archegos sell-off exposed the fragility of the financial system, especially those involving lesser-known practices such as <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/sec-action-bill-hwang-archegos-implosion-experts-say-13fs-swaps-2021-3?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestgest\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">total return swaps</a>, a derivative instrument that enabled Bill Hwang&#39;s office not to have ownership of the underlying securities his firm was betting on and the secrecy of family offices. Typically, family offices enjoy the &#34;private adviser exemption&#34; provided under the Advisers Act to firms as these usually advise less than 15 clients, among other conditions.</p><p>But JPMorgan said, &#34;filing requirements would have applied to Archegos given its sizable exposure to some US securities. However, the fact that Archegos did not file with the [Securities and Exchange Commission] can be explained by the usage of total return swaps, which seems to be the primary method through which the sizable positions were built by Archegos.&#34;</p><p>Dan Berkovitz, a Democratic commissioner on the Commodity Futures Trading Commission, denounced family offices and their ability to skirt some oversight.</p><p>&#34;A &#39;family office&#39; has nothing to do with ordinary families,&#34; he <a href=\"https://www.cftc.gov/PressRoom/SpeechesTestimony/berkovitzstatement040121\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">said in a statement</a> on April 1. &#34;Rather, it is an investment vehicle used by centimillionaires and billionaires to grow their wealth, reduce their taxes, and plan their estates.&#34;</p><p><a href=\"https://www.cftc.gov/PressRoom/SpeechesTestimony/berkovitzstatement040121\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"></a>Third, JPMorgan said private banks, specifically those linked to Archegos, moving forward could improve their onboarding, especially with clients with backgrounds such as Hwang, who has <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/who-is-bill-hwang-archegos-tiger-cub-christian-insider-trading-2021-3-1030255723?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestgest\">run into trouble in the past</a>. Private banks could also strengthen their risk management by giving less leverage to non-transparent family offices with concentrated positions and ensure checking the clients&#39; <a href=\"https://repowatch.files.wordpress.com/2012/01/jpmtripartyrehypo.pdf\">rehypothecation risk</a>, among others.</p><p>Archegos in late March used borrowed money to make large bets on some stocks until Wall Street banks forced Archegos to sell over $20 billion worth of its shares after failing to meet a margin call. Hwang grew his family office&#39;s $200 million investment to $10 billion. Reports say the former Tiger Management trader <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/bill-hwang-lost-8-billion-during-archegos-meltdown-reports-say-2021-4-1030272030\">lost $8 billion in 10 days.</a></p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a compensation for the second half of 2020.</p>\n<p>Those bonuses, awarded at 110%, were at least partially tied to \"election integrity efforts in connection with the U.S. 2020 elections,\" <a href=\"https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1326801/000132680121000022/facebook2021definitiveprox.htm\">according to an SEC filing</a> from the company first spotted by The Information.</p>\n<p>Ahead of the November 2020 elections, Facebook rolled out a number of measures intended to curb misinformation and promote voting.</p>\n<p>The company <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/facebook-adds-label-to-posts-about-voting-from-politicians-2020-7\">added labels</a> to all posts about voting that came from federal elected officials and candidates, it <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/facebook-says-it-will-ban-all-political-ads-after-election-2020-10\">paused political ad buying</a> for months, and opened an information center intended to inform users about voting laws. Those efforts were apparently considered a success if the bonus payouts are any indication.</p>\n<p><em><strong>Read more: </strong><a 'toxic positivity,' where workers feel pressure to share personal information with managers and constant feedback can feel like bullying</a></em></p>\n<p>In the years following the 2016 US presidential election, Facebook struggled with how to moderate speech and advertising from politicians and political campaigns.</p>\n<p>CEO Mark Zuckerberg has remained steadfast in his argument that political advertising is equivalent to political speech, and that political speech shouldn't be moderated by the social media giant.</p>\n<p>\"In a democracy it's really important that people can see for themselves what politicians are saying so they can make their own judgments,\" Zuckerberg said in a late 2019 interview <a href=\"https://www.cbsnews.com/news/facebook-ceo-mark-zuckerberg-political-ads-people-should-judge-for-themselves-the-character-of-politicians/\">with CBS This Morning cohost Gayle King</a>. \"I don't think that a private company should be censoring politicians or news.\"</p>\n<p>Following the 2020 US election, as former President Donald Trump repeatedly insisted that the election had been \"stolen\" and Trump supporters stormed the US Capitol building, Facebook took the unprecedented step of <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/facebook-has-blocked-president-trump-indefinitely-2021-1\">outright banning Trump</a> from its platforms. </p>\n<p>\"The shocking events of the last 24 hours clearly demonstrate that President Donald Trump intends to use his remaining time in office to undermine the peaceful and lawful transition of power to his elected successor, Joe Biden,\" <a would say support from Republicans and Democrats,&#34; Anita Dunn <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/politics/biden-bipartisan/2021/04/11/65b29ad8-96f0-11eb-b28d-bfa7bb5cb2a5_story.html?utm_source=feedly&utm_medium=referral&utm_campaign=wp_politicspolitics\">told the Washington Post</a>. &#34;It doesn&#39;t say the Republicans have to be in Congress.&#34;</p><p>Another senior Biden advisor echoed that. &#34;The Biden definition of bipartisanship is an agenda that unifies the country and appeals across the political spectrum,&#34; Mike Donilon told the Post. &#34;I think it&#39;s a pretty good definition to say you&#39;re pursuing an agenda that will unite the country, that will bring Democrats and Republicans together across the country.&#34;</p><p>Their remarks are a fresh sign the Biden administration is prepared to claim a bipartisan victory on another part of their legislative agenda once it is passed, even if it does not secure a single Republican vote in Congress. Biden&#39;s $1.9 trillion stimulus plan last month <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stimulus-no-republican-in-congress-voted-for-biden-stimulus-package-2021-3\">did not garner any GOP support</a> in either chamber.</p><p>Parts of that package, though, like its $1,400 direct payments, <a href=\"https://morningconsult.com/2021/03/17/stimulus-package-measures-support-poll/\">were popular</a> with Republican voters.</p><p>Biden&#39;s $2.3 trillion infrastructure plan includes major funding to repair roads and bridges and set up clean energy incentives. It also includes federal dollars for in-home elder care, public transit, broadband, and schools, among others. The White House wants to finance it through corporate tax hikes.</p><p>Segments of the infrastructure plan <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/what-do-republican-voters-think-of-bidens-infrastructure-proposals-poll-2021-4\">have drawn</a> support from Republicans - Republican voters, that is. A strong majority of Republican voters <a href=\"https://twitter.com/josephzeballos/status/1379973725957857281?s=20\">backed the care-giving provision</a> for elderly and disabled people in the plan, per <a href=\"https://assets.morningconsult.com/wp-uploads/2021/04/07082047/210419_crosstabs_POLITICO_RVs_v1_SH.pdf\">a recent Politico/Morning Consult poll.</a> They are more divided on paying for it with tax increases on multinational companies.</p><p>Biden is set to meet with a bipartisan group of eight lawmakers on Monday about his infrastructure plan. But Democrats say they are prepared to bypass the GOP using budget reconciliation if a deal fails to materialize.</p><p>&#34;Our hand is extended. Let&#39;s find out where we can find our common ground,&#34; House speaker Nancy Pelosi told CBS News on Sunday. &#34;We always have a responsibility to strive for bipartisanship.&#34;</p><div class=\"copyright\">Seminole County</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Embattled former Florida tax collector Joel Greenberg had a troubled tenure in office, the NYT reports.</li>\n<li>Before being charged with federal crimes, Greenberg was accused of abusing his office and wasting taxpayer funds.</li>\n<li>Greenberg, an associate of Rep. Matt Gaetz, is now a key player in the investigation against Gaetz.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Embattled former Seminole County, Florida, tax collector Joel Greenberg, now a major player in the federal probe against Rep. Matt Gaetz, had a long history of being accused of official misconduct, including having complaints filed accusing him of acting as a vigilante police officer and abusing taxpayer funds.</p><p>A new examination of Greenberg&#39;s tenure <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2021/04/11/us/politics/joel-greenberg-matt-gaetz.html?referringSource=articleShare\">by The New York Times on Sunday</a> uncovered numerous warning signs about Greenberg&#39;s conduct in office. After a somewhat troubled youth, Greenberg was elected to the position of tax collector in the county in 2016, defeating the longtime incumbent in a primary challenge. </p><p>The Times said that Greenberg soon found himself &#34;bored&#34; in his position and sought ways to use it to his personal advantage, a tendency that would lead to his downfall and formal resignation in 2020.</p><p><em><strong>Read more: </strong></em><em><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/matt-gaetz-trump-fundraising-federal-investigation-2021-04\" data-analytics-position=\"6\" data-analytics-module=\"\">Matt Gaetz is employing Trump-style fundraising pitches - including automatic recurring contributions that could dupe donors</a></em></p><p>In a complaint filed with the county&#39;s sheriff&#39;s office and <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2021/04/11/us/politics/joel-greenberg-matt-gaetz.html?referringSource=articleShare\">reported by The Times</a>, a woman said that in 2017 Greenberg pulled her over with his tax collector&#39;s badge around his neck and yelled at her for apparently cutting him off, saying she drove &#34;like a bat out of hell.&#34; </p><p>Greenberg was also accused of spending over $1 million in taxpayer funds for gear including weapons and a drone, creating &#34;no-show jobs&#34; for family and members of his wedding party, and of trying to solicit help to hack computers belonging to member of the county commission who had criticized him.</p><p>One lawyer who represented one of Greenberg&#39;s former employees <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2021/04/11/us/politics/joel-greenberg-matt-gaetz.html?referringSource=articleShare\">told The Times</a>: &#34;It&#39;s like the Tiger King got elected tax collector.&#34;</p><p>Greenberg was also accused of stalking Brian Beute, a rival Democratic candidate for tax collector and local music teacher who had advocated against a real estate development supported by Greenberg. </p><p>Greenberg&#39;s actions, according to the indictment against him, included sending a fake anonymous letter to the school claiming Beute had an inappropriate relationship with a student (and making a Facebook account to perpetuate the same false claims) and creating a fake Twitter account posing as Beutler that posted white supremacist and pro-segregationist content.  </p><p>Greenberg was first criminally charged with stalking Beute and unlawfully using his identity by federal prosecutors <a href=\"https://www.justice.gov/usao-mdfl/press-release/file/1287831/download\">in June 2020</a>.</p><p>He now faces over 30 federal charges ranging from sex trafficking of a child, aggravated identity theft, creating fake IDs, wire fraud, illegal monetary transfers, and conspiracy to bribe a public official to defraud the Small Business Administration for COVID-19 relief funds, <a href=\"https://www.orlandosentinel.com/news/crime/os-prem-ne-joel-greenberg-federal-charges-case-matt-gaetz-20210410-4jhaknsbxjgv5h5zk2i3kqd2v4-story.html\">according to the Orlando Sentinel. </a></p><p>The Sentinel reports that Greenberg <a href=\"https://www.orlandosentinel.com/news/crime/os-prem-ne-joel-greenberg-federal-charges-case-matt-gaetz-20210410-4jhaknsbxjgv5h5zk2i3kqd2v4-story.html\">is expected to strike a plea deal</a> to plead guilty to some of the charges by mid-May. Greenberg faces 10 years to life in prison on the sex trafficking charge alone. </p><p>During his tumultuous tenure as tax collector, Greenberg became friends with Gaetz, and was brought into the fray of Republican politics in the state. He even spoke at a rally for former President Donald Trump in 2016.</p><p>Gaetz <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/prosecutors-doj-veterans-react-joel-greenberg-plea-deal-matt-gaetz-2021-4\">is under federal investigation</a> for possible trafficking of a minor across state lines. Authorities are also examining whether Gaetz and Greenberg paid women for sex. Gaetz has denied the accusations and has not yet been charged with a crime. </p><p><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/prosecutors-doj-veterans-react-joel-greenberg-plea-deal-matt-gaetz-2021-4\">Insider&#39;s Sonam Sheth recently reported</a> that the extent of Greenberg&#39;s current legal woes could up the pressure on him to cooperate against others involved in the charges against him, and potentially cause serious trouble for Gaetz.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Goldman&#39;s international asset management CEO broke down four key themes retail investors should look out for.</li>\n<li>He sees opportunities in ESG, healthcare, rel=\"noopener\">Sign up here for our daily newsletter, 10 Things Before the Opening Bell.</a></strong></li>\n</ul><p>The pandemic has accelerated a number of trends in the stock market that retail investors should keep an eye out for, Goldman&#39;s Fadi Abuali said in a recent in-house interview.</p><p>As the CEO of Goldman Sachs Asset Management International, Abuali oversees work with both large institutional investor clients and third party banks who are wholesalers to retail investors. </p><p>Here are four megatrends Goldman Sachs Asset Management is focused on in the retail investing space: </p><p><strong>Thinking like a Millennial </strong></p><p>How the largest generation in mankind&#39;s history saves their money, spends their money, and uses their time is a major area of focus for Goldman, Abuali said. </p><p>&#34;They&#39;re tech enabled. They prefer healthy lifestyles. Focus on sustainable living. They also prefer experiences over things,&#34; said Abuali, who suggests investors seek out companies that will benefit from millennials&#39; lifestyle choices. <br><br><strong>The Green Revolution </strong><br><br>ESG investing  is also a major area of focus for Abuali, particularly because many COVID-related fiscal packages in economies across the globe include funding for renewable power, electric vehicles, and grid infrastructure.</p><p>Abuali said companies that invest in automation efficiencies, renewables, sustainable transportation, recycling, packaging, and plant-based proteins  will &#34;generate meaningful alpha opportunities&#34; for Goldman and their clients.</p><p><strong>Emerging Technology </strong></p><p>While technology was an attractive area of the market through the COVID-19 crisis, the demand for working online and transacting remotely won&#39;t die down after the pandemic, Abuali said.</p><p>He said investors should seek out companies that involve cybersecurity, software as a service, cloud platforms, and data centers. These will all be &#34;big beneficiaries&#34; of the continued trend of working from home. </p><p><strong>Healthcare </strong><br><br>Abuali also sees key opportunities in the healthcare industry as a result of the coordination between the private sector and public sector during the pandemic.</p><p>&#34;..With greater regulatory comfort and better access to capital, we expect to see the emergence of many new solutions for challenging diseases,&#34; said the CEO. &#34;And so, genomics, gene sequencing, gene editing, gene therapy, and concerning supply side manufacturing opportunities will href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/stocks/apha-stock\">Aphria</a> saw its stock fall as much as 15% on Monday after the company turned in a disappointing fiscal third-quarter earnings report.</p><p>Aphria said it generated revenue of $153.6 million in the quarter compared to analysts&#39; consensus expectations for $163.3 million. Revenue was up 6.4% year-over-year, but sequentially it fell 4.3%.</p><p>Adjusted EBITDA came in at $12.7 million in the fiscal third quarter, which was below analyst consensus estimates for $14.9 million.</p><p>Aphria also turned in a record net loss of $361.0 million, or $1.14 per share, in the fiscal third quarter. The loss was partly due to increasing production costs at the firm.</p><p>The cash cost to produce dried cannabis per gram rose 13.9% quarter-over-quarter in Aphria&#39;s quarterly report and adjusted cannabis gross margin sank to 39.2% vs. 45.9% in the prior quarter.</p><p>The Leamington, Canada-based company saw its cannabis sales fall to 18,695 kgs in the fiscal third quarter as well, a decrease of 30.1% compared to the fiscal second quarter.</p><p>The company&#39;s CEO Irwin Simon pointed to the impact of lockdowns as his reasoning for the disappointing results.  </p><p>&#34;The duration and impact of lockdowns across many of the regions we operate in, particularly in Canada, were greater than we initially anticipated for the cannabis industry and our business; however, we believe Aphria remains well-positioned with our leading brands and market share to experience a robust increase in our top-line as the market improves.&#34; the chief executive officer wrote in a <a href=\"https://www.prnewswire.com/news-releases/aphria-inc-announces-third-quarter-fiscal-year-2021-results-301266533.html\">press release</a>.</p><p>One bright spot in the fiscal third-quarter report came from SweetWater, a cannabis brewing company Aphria acquired for $300 million in 2020. SweetWater saw sales climb from $0.9 million last quarter to nearly $15 million. </p><p>Aphria is set to merge with fellow cannabis producer <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/stocks/tlry-stock\">Tilray</a> to make the largest cannabis company in the world in terms of revenue pending shareholder approval on Wednesday.  </p><p>The combined company has plans to continue its international expansion as the regulatory environment for cannabis producers and retailers continues to improve. </p><p>Aphria traded down 14.71% as of 11:49 href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>President Joe Biden probably won&#39;t get everything on his economic-recovery wish list, Goldman Sachs economists said Sunday.</p><p>Despite passing $1.9 trillion in new stimulus just one month ago, the White House is already prepping another massive spending effort. The president <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/biden-2-trillion-infrastructure-plan-roads-bridges-broadband-power-2021-3\">unveiled the $2.3 trillion American Jobs Plan</a> in late March and is reportedly teeing up another package that would set the two plans&#39; combined price tag <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/biden-infrastructure-bill-package-4-trillion-with-tax-hikes-2021-3\">at roughly $4 trillion</a>. Biden also proposed a handful of tax hikes set to offset the spending measures&#39; hefty costs.</p><p>The White House will be successful in pushing the proposals over the finish line, but the policies won&#39;t look the same once they get there, economists led by Jan Hatzius said in a note to clients. Congress will pass nearly all of the &#34;hard infrastructure&#34; Biden has proposed, such as road and bridge renovation, updates to federal buildings, and clean water initiatives, they added.</p><p>Biden&#39;s American Families Plan won&#39;t be so untouched. The bank said Congress will likely approve the package - which is expected to include spending on education and child care - but only as a slimmed-down version. All told, it sees about $3.3 trillion in spending making its way back to Biden&#39;s desk for his final signature, roughly $700 billion less than the president pushed for.</p><p>The Biden administration won&#39;t see its tax plans come to fruition, either. Congressional Democrats will end up lifting the corporate tax rate to 25% from 21%, Goldman said, coming in shy of the 28% rate sought by the president.</p><p>The tax rate for long-term capital gains and qualified dividends will reach 28%, the team added. That compares to the 39.6% rate proposed by the White House. Revenue raised by international tax proposals like a global minimum corporate tax rate would likely be cut in half, according to the bank.</p><p>The smaller tax hikes and cuts to spending stand to garner additional support for the two plans, but Democrats are still most likely to lump the proposals together and pass them through reconciliation, Goldman said. The process was used for Biden&#39;s stimulus plan and allows Democrats to approve legislation with a simple majority.</p><p>The bank&#39;s most probable scenario sees Democrats skirting GOP opposition and passing a single package through budget reconciliation sometime between July and September. The plan will cost roughly $3.5 trillion over the next 10 years and will be partially offset by $1.5 trillion in new tax revenues over the same period, the team said. </p><p>&#34;The risk in this approach is that some centrist Democrats might balk at passing such a large bill through the reconciliation process, which could delay or potentially imperil passage,&#34; they added.</p><p>Other, less likely options include passing two separate bills through reconciliation and passing three measures through a mix of reconciliation votes and regular Congressional process. Pursuing regular votes would likely punt some bills into the fall as Democrats work to win Republican support, according to Goldman.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a Winck)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/6074511f2cc1ef0018da1311?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn100@2020_2/2021/04/12/16-44-11-877_f226999551d0b9be.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn39@2020_2/2021/04/12/16-44-16-326_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=sc-vuoVHKVc:P6KrbfWjsSI:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn2@2020_6/2021/04/12/16-44-18-264_e5328066b4eb7a7e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=sc-vuoVHKVc:P6KrbfWjsSI:V_sGLiPBpWU":null,"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn51@2020_2/2021/04/12/16-44-13-888_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=sc-vuoVHKVc:P6KrbfWjsSI:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn28@2020_5/2021/04/12/16-44-12-622_89f69cf000079cd0.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/sc-vuoVHKVc":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn60@2020_1/2021/04/12/16-44-14-441_2c0beb003bfd494d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1618245786307},{"createdTime":"2021-04-13 00:43:06","updatedTime":"2021-04-13 00:43:06","title":"A new DEA map shows where cartels have influence in the US. Cartel operatives say 'it's bulls---'","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/cartel-operatives-criticize-dea-map-of-cartel-influence-in-us-2021-4","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5beef0d7bde70f2f8b3d7daa?format=jpeg\" alt=\"drugs mexico cartels\" height=\"1750\" width=\"3500\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Mexican soldiers stand guard next to packages of marijuana at a military base in Tijuana, June 13, 2015.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Reuters</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>The Drug Enforcement Administration&#39;s latest report on illicit drugs and drug trafficking details what the agency says is cartel influence in the US.</li>\n<li>Security experts and cartel operatives in Mexico dispute the DEA&#39;s depiction, however, arguing the links are more tenuous than the DEA describes them. </li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p align=\"left\">Ciudad Juarez, MEXICO - The US Drug Enforcement Administration (DEA) recently released its <a href=\"https://www.dea.gov/sites/default/files/2021-02/DIR-008-21%202020%20National%20Drug%20Threat%20Assessment_WEB.pdf\">an</a><a href=\"https://www.dea.gov/sites/default/files/2021-02/DIR-008-21%202020%20National%20Drug%20Threat%20Assessment_WEB.pdf\">n</a><a href=\"https://www.dea.gov/sites/default/files/2021-02/DIR-008-21%202020%20National%20Drug%20Threat%20Assessment_WEB.pdf\">ual National Drug Threat Assessment</a>, in which it maps out the states where Mexican drug cartels have gained &#34;influence.&#34;</p><p align=\"left\">Asked about that depiction of cartel presence in the US, security experts and cartel sources told Insider &#34;it&#39;s bullshit.&#34;</p><p align=\"left\">The DEA&#39;s report says Mexican transnational criminal organizations, or TCOs, &#34;maintain great influence&#34; in most US states, with the Sinaloa Cartel and Cartel Jalisco Nueva Generacion showing the &#34;biggest signs of expansion.&#34;</p><p align=\"left\">A map included in the report shows the Sinaloa Cartel, Cartel Jalisco Nueva Generacion, Cartel del Golfo, Organización de Beltran-Leyva, and Los Rojos as the most &#34;influential&#34; drug organizations with presence in Texas, California, Arizona, New Mexico, Chicago, New York, Florida, Kansas, Colorado, Hawaii and Puerto Rico, among other states.</p><p align=\"left\">&#34;Mexican TCOs continue to control lucrative smuggling corridors, primarily across the SWB [Southwest Border], and maintain the greatest drug trafficking influence in the United States,&#34; the report says.</p><p align=\"left\"></p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/60739696a451820019b3a78a?format=jpeg\" alt=\"DEA map cartel influence in US\" height=\"1148\" width=\"2054\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Major Mexican organized-crime groups&#39; areas of influence in the US, according to the DEA&#39;s 2020 National Drug Threat Assessment.\n<p class=\"copyright\">2020 National Drug Threat Assessment</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p align=\"left\">But operatives for the Sinaloa Cartel and Cartel Jalisco Nueva Generacion interviewed by Insider said their criminal organizations maintain &#34;only clients or helpers&#34; across the border and &#34;not members of our organization.&#34;</p><p align=\"left\">&#34;You would never see anyone in the US saying they are part of the organization [Sinaloa Cartel], because that is bulls---. The members and leaders of the organization are in Mexico, not in the US. What we have there are clients or associates, people helping transport, or gang members working with us,&#34; a Sinaloa Cartel operative told Insider.</p><p align=\"left\">The operative explained that most of the gangs or &#34;associates&#34; in the US work as independents.</p><p align=\"left\">&#34;We wholesale to them and what they do to that merchandise is their problem. We don&#39;t give a f---. They can loose it, sell it, snort it, whatever, as long as they pay up,&#34; he said.</p><p align=\"left\">One of the most prominent cases used to <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/what-are-mexican-cartels-doing-in-the-us-2017-4\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">prove Mexican cartels&#39; presence in the US</a> was that of Pedro and Margarito Flores, two brothers from Chicago accused of importing cocaine for the Sinaloa Cartel.</p><p align=\"left\"></p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/54c6b61569bedd943bbb291e?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Pedro and Margarito Flores\" height=\"1258\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Pedro Flores, left, and his twin brother, Margarito Flores, in undated photos from a wanted poster released by the US Marshals Service.\n<p class=\"copyright\">AP Photo/U.S. Marshals Service</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p align=\"left\">The Flores brothers admitted to smuggling at least 1,500 kgs of cocaine for the Sinaloa cartel into the US every month between 2005 and 2008. <a href=\"https://www.justice.gov/opa/pr/chicago-twins-cooperation-against-sinaloa-cartel-yields-14-year-prison-terms-new-charges\">According to their guilty pleas</a>, they also sent more than $930 million in &#34;bulk cash&#34; back to the cartel in Mexico.</p><p align=\"left\">US authorities allege the brothers were part of the Sinaloa Cartel, but a phone call of a negotiation with then-Sinaloa Cartel leader Joaquin &#34;El Chapo&#34; Guzman that was <a href=\"https://www.rollingstone.com/culture/culture-news/el-chapo-pedro-flores-trial-trafficker-772056/\">made public during </a><a href=\"https://www.rollingstone.com/culture/culture-news/el-chapo-pedro-flores-trial-trafficker-772056/\">Guzman</a><a href=\"https://www.rollingstone.com/culture/culture-news/el-chapo-pedro-flores-trial-trafficker-772056/\">&#39;s trial in 2018</a> revealed Flores brothers bargaining over the price of a 20 kg shipment of heroin.</p><p align=\"left\">&#34;Do you think we can work something out where you can deduct five pesos from those for me?&#34; said a man identified as Pedro Flores.</p><p align=\"left\">&#34;How much are you going to pay for it?&#34; said the other man on the call, allegedly Guzman.</p><h2 align=\"left\">&#39;It just doesn&#39;t make sense&#39;</h2><p align=\"left\"></p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5d0cfaa5e3ecba3afe41cc42?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Philadelphia cocaine drug bust\" height=\"933\" width=\"1400\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A fraction of the cocaine seized from a ship at a Philadelphia port on display at the US Custom House in Philadelphia, June 21, 2019.\n<p class=\"copyright\">AP Photo/Matt Rourke</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p align=\"left\">Alejandro Hope, a security analyst in Mexico and former official with CISEN, Mexico&#39;s top security intelligence organization, said the DEA warns of Mexican drug cartels being active in the US in order &#34;to keep asking for money.&#34;</p><p align=\"left\">&#34;It&#39;s DEA&#39;s bulls---. They have been doing this for years, and it just doesn&#39;t make sense. Cartels today are not structured [like] a hierarchy organization, but more like a decentralized network,&#34; Hope told Insider.</p><p align=\"left\">&#34;The logic behind the DEA [report] is to argue there is an invasion of external forces so they can justify more budget and support from the US,&#34; he said.</p><p align=\"left\">Neither DEA headquarters nor its offices in Texas and Arizona responded to requests for comment on the map.</p><p align=\"left\">The report describes the Cartel Jalisco Nueva Generacion as &#34;one of the fastest growing cartels&#34; and says the organization &#34;smuggles illicit drugs into the United States by accessing various trafficking corridors in northern Mexico along the SWB including Tijuana, Juarez and Nuevo Laredo.&#34;</p><p align=\"left\">&#34;The cartels dominate the drug trade influencing the United States market, with most cartels having a poly drug market approach that allows for maximum flexibility and resiliency of their operations,&#34; the report states.</p><p align=\"left\">The report doesn&#39;t describe how these organizations maintain their presence in the US.</p><p align=\"left\">&#34;The DEA has a problem with semantics. What does influence actually mean? What does presence even mean? An associate is no other thing but a client,&#34; Hope said.</p><p align=\"left\"></p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5852f090a1a45e8b3f8b532f?format=jpeg\" alt=\"US drug market mexican cartel control DEA map\" height=\"790\" width=\"984\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The cartel areas of influence map from the 2016 National Drug Threat Assessment.\n<p class=\"copyright\">DEA NDTA 2016</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p align=\"left\">An operative for Cartel Jalisco Nueva Generacion said the organization maintained a large group of members in Mexico who are &#34;mostly on the armed side of the operations,&#34; while most contacts in the US were clients.</p><p align=\"left\">&#34;Most of what we can call members of the Jalisco organization are on the arms [side], like sicarios, and some producers that are on a payroll, but everyone else is either a client we are selling to or an association to have access to certain route&#34; for distribution in the US, he said.</p><p align=\"left\">Some intelligence officials believe Mexican cartels do have a real presence on US soil but function differently there.</p><p align=\"left\">&#34;The substantial difference is that drug criminal enterprises are not displaying force at the border with the US because it is not needed. We should take into account that keeping a low profile is good for their activities and business, just as any other corporation,&#34; said a high-level foreign intelligence official in Mexico who asked for anonymity.</p><p align=\"left\">The official said cartel associates in the US have something like membership, even if they aren&#39;t part of the cartel structure, and &#34;are using the brand&#34; to prove their drugs&#39; quality.</p><p align=\"left\">&#34;We need to consider that they act just as another transnational company, with their level of organization, distribution, reach, and territory control. They do have a presence in the US in how their drug has a brand backing up certain quality,&#34; the official said.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a
